"""Numerically tight softmax via Triton with CUDA graph replay.

Two Triton kernels + CUDA graph capture for minimal launch overhead:

  Kernel 1 (block_stats): One program per (row, col_block). Each finds its
             local max and sum-of-exp(x - local_max), writes to scratch.
  Kernel 2 (output):       One program per (row, col_block). Merges all
             per-block stats via online reduction with Kahan compensation,
             then writes exp(x - global_max) / global_sum.

On first call the kernels are JIT-compiled, then a CUDA graph is captured.
Subsequent calls copy input to a static buffer and replay the graph,
eliminating Python overhead and kernel launch latency.
"""
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import triton
import triton.language as tl


@triton.jit
def _block_stats_kernel(
    X_ptr,
    max_scratch,
    sum_scratch,
    batch,
    vocab,
    stride_xb,
    stride_xv,
    BLOCK: tl.constexpr,
    NUM_BLOCKS: tl.constexpr,
):
    pid = tl.program_id(0)
    row = pid // NUM_BLOCKS
    blk = pid % NUM_BLOCKS
    if row >= batch:
        return

    start = blk * BLOCK
    cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK)
    c = start + cols
    m = c < vocab

    x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf"))
    lm = tl.max(x)
    e = tl.exp(x - lm)
    ls = tl.sum(e)

    tl.store(max_scratch + pid, lm)
    tl.store(sum_scratch + pid, ls)


@triton.jit
def _output_kernel(
    X_ptr,
    Y_ptr,
    max_scratch,
    sum_scratch,
    batch,
    vocab,
    stride_xb,
    stride_xv,
    stride_yb,
    stride_yv,
    BLOCK: tl.constexpr,
    NUM_BLOCKS: tl.constexpr,
):
    pid = tl.program_id(0)
    row = pid // NUM_BLOCKS
    blk = pid % NUM_BLOCKS
    if row >= batch:
        return

    # --- Online merge of per-block (max, sum) with Kahan compensation ---
    gmax = -float("inf")
    gsum = 0.0
    kc = 0.0

    for i in range(NUM_BLOCKS):
        lm = tl.load(max_scratch + row * NUM_BLOCKS + i)
        ls = tl.load(sum_scratch + row * NUM_BLOCKS + i)

        old = gmax
        gmax = tl.maximum(gmax, lm)
        corr = tl.exp(old - gmax)
        gsum = gsum * corr
        kc = kc * corr

        corrected = ls * tl.exp(lm - gmax)
        y = corrected - kc
        t = gsum + y
        kc = (t - gsum) - y
        gsum = t

    inv = 1.0 / gsum

    # --- Write normalized output ---
    start = blk * BLOCK
    cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK)
    c = start + cols
    m = c < vocab

    x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0)
    out = tl.exp(x - gmax) * inv
    tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, out, mask=m)


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch: int, vocab: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.batch = batch
        self.vocab = vocab
        self._graph = None

    def _run_kernels(self, x, y, nb, total):
        _block_stats_kernel[(total,)](
            x, self._max_s, self._sum_s,
            self.batch, self.vocab,
            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
            BLOCK=8192,
            NUM_BLOCKS=nb,
            num_warps=8,
        )
        _output_kernel[(total,)](
            x, y, self._max_s, self._sum_s,
            self.batch, self.vocab,
            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
            y.stride(0), y.stride(1),
            BLOCK=8192,
            NUM_BLOCKS=nb,
            num_warps=8,
        )

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        if self._graph is None:
            BLOCK = 8192
            nb = triton.cdiv(self.vocab, BLOCK)
            total = self.batch * nb

            self._static_x = x
            self._y = torch.empty_like(x)
            self._max_s = torch.empty(total, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
            self._sum_s = torch.empty(total, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)

            # Triton JIT warmup
            for _ in range(5):
                self._run_kernels(self._static_x, self._y, nb, total)
            torch.cuda.synchronize()

            # Capture CUDA graph
            self._graph = torch.cuda.CUDAGraph()
            with torch.cuda.graph(self._graph):
                self._run_kernels(self._static_x, self._y, nb, total)
            return self._y

        if x.data_ptr() != self._static_x.data_ptr():
            self._static_x.copy_(x)
        self._graph.replay()
        return self._y


BATCH = 8
VOCAB = 32768


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(BATCH, VOCAB, dtype=torch.float32) * 4.0]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [BATCH, VOCAB]

Total traffic = 2 reads + 1 write - **CUDA graph capture**: first call JIT-compiles and captures a graph; subsequent calls replay with zero-copy when the input address matches, eliminating Python and kernel launch overhead - **Kahan compensation** in the cross-block sum merge keeps the 256K-vocab shape under 1e-5 tolerance

21	def _make_inputs(batch: int, vocab: int, extreme: bool, seed: int) -> torch.Tensor:
22	    g = torch.Generator().manual_seed(seed)
23	    if extreme:
24	        # Adversarial: most logits are mild but a handful per row are huge.
25	        # If the kernel forgets to subtract the row-max before exp, this
26	        # row overflows fp32 and produces NaN/Inf. If it accumulates in
27	        # fp16, the long tail of small exp() values is lost beneath the
28	        # tolerance threshold.
29	        x = torch.randn(batch, vocab, generator=g) * 2.0
30	        # Spike: 4 very large positive logits per row.

33	    else:

1	"""Naive softmax over the last dim, computed in fp64 for ground-truth.
2	
3	The reference deliberately runs in float64 so that fp16 / fp32 accumulation
4	drift in agent solutions is exposed by the tight tolerance in problem.yaml.
5	The agent's job is to produce an fp32 softmax whose values match this
6	double-precision reference within atol=rtol=1e-5 — this requires either
7	fp32 accumulation or compensated (Kahan) summation when vocab is large.
8	"""
9	import torch
10	import torch.nn as nn
11	
12	OP_TYPE = "softmax"
13	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
14	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
15	
16	
17	class Model(nn.Module):
18	    """y = softmax(x, dim=-1) computed in fp64 then returned as fp32.
19	
20	    No learned parameters — softmax is parameter-free. We still expose an
21	    empty state_dict so the harness's strict load_state_dict matches.
22	    """
23	
24	    def __init__(self, batch: int, vocab: int):
25	        super().__init__()
26	        self.batch = batch
27	        self.vocab = vocab
28	
29	    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
30	        # Promote to fp64 for the ground-truth pathway. Even with double
31	        # precision we still subtract the row-max for stability.
32	        x64 = x.to(torch.float64)
33	        m = x64.amax(dim=-1, keepdim=True)
34	        e = torch.exp(x64 - m)
35	        s = e.sum(dim=-1, keepdim=True)
36	        return (e / s).to(torch.float32)

1	"""Shape sweep for Kahan-corrected softmax.
2	
3	The point of this problem is numerical accuracy on long reductions. Shapes
4	mix typical LLM vocab sizes with deliberately adversarial regimes:
5	
6	  - small vocab (sanity check; naive fp32 should pass)
7	  - Llama3 vocab 128K (real-world, where fp16 accumulation starts to drift)
8	  - 256K (DeepSeek-V3 / Gemma-3 class vocab; naive fp16 sum DOES drift past
9	    the 1e-5 tolerance — this row is what proves Kahan was needed)
10	  - extreme-logit edge case (large positive logits stress max-subtract +
11	    summation; if the implementation accidentally exps before subtracting
12	    max, this row overflows)
13	
14	The 'extreme' flag is read by check.py to switch input generation to a
15	distribution that produces a few very large logits per row.
16	"""

reasoning (61759 chars)
Now I have a clear picture of the problem. Let me analyze what's needed: 1. **Softmax along last dim of 2D fp32 tensor** - memory-bound operation 2. **Tight tolerance**: atol=rtol=1e-5 (tighter than default fp32 1e-4) 3. **Shapes**: up to 256K vocab, which means long reductions where fp16 accumulation drifts 4. **Reference**: Computes in fp64 for ground truth 5. **Need**: fp32 accumulation with subtract-max stability, possibly Kahan summation for large vocabs 6. **Forbidden**: Can't use torch.softmax, F.softmax, liger_kernel softmax, or .softmax( This is memory-bound (softmax reads input once, writes output once), so the ceiling is ~1.8 TB/s bandwidth. The key challenge is numerical accuracy at 1e-5 tolerance across 256K element reductions. Let me think about the kernel approach: **Triton kernel** seems like the best approach - it's the most productive for this kind of element-wise + reduction operation. The kernel needs to: 1. Find the row max (with fp32 accumulation) 2. Subtract max from each element 3. Exp each element 4. Sum the exps (with Kahan/compensated summation for large vocabs) 5. Divide each element by the sum For the Kahan summation on large reductions (256K), we need: - A running compensation term that captures the low-order bits lost in fp32 addition Let me think about the Triton implementation: ```python @triton.jit def softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_x_batch, stride_x_vocab, stride_y_batch, stride_y_vocab, BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): row_idx = tl.program_id(0) if row_idx >= batch: return row_start = row_idx * stride_x_batch # Pass 1: Find max max_val = -float('inf') for block_start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offsets = block_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) mask = offsets < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row_start + offsets * stride_x_vocab, mask=mask, other=-float('inf')) max_val = tl.maximum(max_val, tl.max(x, axis=0)) # Broadcast max_val for pass 2 # Pass 2: exp(x - max) and sum # For Kahan summation: sum_val = 0.0 compensation = 0.0 # We need to store exp values too, so we need a third pass or use scratch # Actually, let me think about this differently. # With Triton, we can do it in 2 passes over the data: # Pass 1: find max # Pass 2: compute exp(x - max), store to Y, accumulate sum with Kahan # Pass 3: divide by sum # But that's 3 passes. For a memory-bound operation, minimizing passes is key. # # Actually, we could do it in 2 passes: # Pass 1: find max # Pass 2: exp(x-max), store to Y, accumulate sum # Then a final scalar divide pass # Or we could use a scratch buffer for the exp values. ``` Wait, the challenge is that with vocab up to 256K, we can't load the entire row into registers at once in a single Triton program. We need to loop over blocks. Let me think about the structure more carefully: For a memory-bound softmax kernel: - **Pass 1**: Find row max by iterating over blocks - **Pass 2**: Compute exp(x - max), write to output, accumulate sum with Kahan - **Pass 3**: Divide each element by sum This is 3 passes over the data = 3x the memory traffic. But since softmax is already memory-bound, this is standard and unavoidable (you need the max before you can safely exp, and you need the sum before you can normalize). Actually, a common optimization is to combine passes 2 and 3 if we have a scratch buffer for the exp values. But that requires an extra buffer. Alternatively, we can do: - Pass 1+2: Find max and compute sum using a two-pass approach in one kernel, but that requires storing intermediates - Or: Online softmax approach where we update max and sum simultaneously **Online softmax**: In the online algorithm, we can compute softmax in a single pass: 1. For each block of elements: a. Find local max b. Update global max c. Update running sum with correction factor exp(old_max - new_max) This requires only 1 pass over the data but involves more computation per element. For memory-bound operations, this doesn't help much since we're already bandwidth-limited. Let me go with the standard 3-pass approach (find max, exp+sum, divide) using Triton. For numerical accuracy with Kahan summation: Actually, let me reconsider. The Triton approach with per-row kernels is clean. Let me structure it as: 1. One kernel per row (or per group of rows) 2. Each kernel loops over blocks to find max 3. Loops again to compute exp and sum (with Kahan) 4. Loops again to divide by sum For the Kahan summation in the sum accumulation: ```python # Kahan summation y = exp_val - compensation t = sum_val + y compensation = (t - sum_val) - y sum_val = t ``` But wait, in Triton, the inner reduction is over a block of elements (e.g., 1024). Within a block, we get SIMD parallelism. The Kahan summation needs to be done sequentially, which doesn't map well to SIMD. Alternative: For the block-level reduction, use fp32 (not fp16). The block size might be 1024 or 2048 elements. Within each block, we compute the sum in fp32 using tree reduction (which Triton does automatically with `tl.sum`). Then we accumulate across blocks using Kahan summation. Actually, the issue is: with 256K elements and BLOCK_SIZE=1024, we have 256 blocks. Accumulating 256 block sums in fp32 should be fine - fp32 has 24 bits of mantissa, so 256 additions shouldn't lose more than ~8 bits of precision, leaving 16 bits. But the exp values can vary enormously, so the relative error might be larger. Let me think about this more carefully. For softmax with 256K vocab: - After subtracting max, values range from 1.0 (the max element) down to exp(-large_value) which can be extremely small - The sum is dominated by a few large values - Small values contribute very little to the sum but still need to be computed accurately With fp32 accumulation: - The sum might be like 10.0 + tiny contributions from many elements - Adding a tiny exp value to a sum of 10.0 loses precision - After 256K such additions, the accumulated error could exceed 1e-5 relative With Kahan summation: - The compensation term captures the lost precision - This should keep the error well within 1e-5 So the approach should be: 1. Find max (fp32, tree reduction per block, then sequential max across blocks) 2. Compute exp(x - max) per block, sum within block (fp32 tree reduction), accumulate across blocks with Kahan 3. Divide each element by sum Let me write the Triton kernel. Actually, I realize I need to be more careful about how Triton handles the per-block reduction. In Triton, `tl.sum(x)` performs a parallel reduction across the SIMD lanes within a block. This is a tree reduction and should be accurate in fp32 for block sizes up to 1024 or so. The key insight is that the Kahan summation is needed for the **cross-block accumulation**, not the intra-block reduction. Within a block of 1024 elements, fp32 tree reduction is fine. But accumulating 256 block sums (for 256K vocab) needs Kahan. Let me write the kernel: ```python import triton import triton.language as tl import torch import torch.nn as nn @triton.jit def softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): row_idx = tl.program_id(0) if row_idx >= batch: return x_row = X_ptr + row_idx * stride_xb y_row = Y_ptr + row_idx * stride_yb # Pass 1: Find row max row_max = -float('inf') num_blocks = tl.cdiv(vocab, BLOCK_SIZE) for b in range(num_blocks): offs = b * BLOCK_SIZE + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(x_row + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=-float('inf')) block_max = tl.max(x, axis=0) row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, block_max) # Pass 2: exp(x - max) and Kahan sum row_sum = 0.0 compensation = 0.0 for b in range(num_blocks): offs = b * BLOCK_SIZE + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(x_row + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=0.0) exp_vals = tl.exp(x - row_max) # Write exp values to output (we'll divide in pass 3) tl.store(y_row + offs * stride_yv, exp_vals, mask=mask) # Sum within block (fp32 tree reduction by Triton) block_sum = tl.sum(exp_vals, axis=0) # Kahan accumulation across blocks y = block_sum - compensation t = row_sum + y compensation = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t # Pass 3: Normalize inv_sum = 1.0 / row_sum for b in range(num_blocks): offs = b * BLOCK_SIZE + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) mask = offs < vocab exp_vals = tl.load(y_row + offs * stride_yv, mask=mask, other=0.0) tl.store(y_row + offs * stride_yv, exp_vals * inv_sum, mask=mask) ``` This does 3 passes over the data. The first pass finds the max, the second computes exp and accumulates the sum with Kahan, and the third normalizes. Wait, I'm storing exp values in Y during pass 2 and then reading them back in pass 3. This means: - Pass 1: Read X (1 read) - Pass 2: Read X, Write Y (1 read, 1 write) - Pass 3: Read Y, Write Y (1 read, 1 write) Total: 2 reads of X, 1 read + 2 writes of Y = 4n reads + 2n writes total bytes = 6 * batch * vocab * 4 bytes. Hmm, that's more than optimal. The theoretical minimum is 1 read + 1 write = 2n * 4 bytes. But for softmax, you always need at least 2 passes (max, then exp/sum/normalize), so you read the data at least twice. The 3rd pass (normalize) reads and writes Y. Can I do better? Let me think... **Online softmax in 1 pass:** Actually, the online softmax algorithm allows computing softmax in a single forward pass by maintaining a running max and a running sum with correction: ``` m_0 = -inf, d_0 = 0 for i = 0 to N-1: m_{i+1} = max(m_i, x_i) d_{i+1} = d_i * exp(m_i - m_{i+1}) + exp(x_i - m_{i+1}) ``` At the end, softmax(x_i) = exp(x_i - m_N) / d_N But this requires updating ALL previously computed values when the max changes, which doesn't work in a streaming fashion unless we store the exp values and go back to fix them. In practice, for a kernel, the standard approach is: 1. One pass to find max 2. One pass to compute exp(x-max) and sum 3. One pass to divide by sum Or with a scratch buffer: 1. Find max 2. Compute exp(x-max) and store, accumulate sum 3. Divide stored values by sum The 3-pass approach is standard and unavoidable for numerically stable softmax. Actually, wait. I can reduce to 2 passes over the input by combining passes differently: **Approach: 2 input passes + 1 output pass** - Pass 1: Read X, find max → write max per row to a small buffer - Pass 2: Read X, compute exp(x-max), accumulate sum → store exp values to Y - Pass 3: Read Y, divide by sum → write back to Y Total: 2 reads of X, 1 read + 2 writes of Y. Since softmax is memory-bound, and Y is the same size as X, this is essentially: - Reads: 2*X + 1*Y = 3 passes of data - Writes: 2*Y = 2 passes of data (but Y is the output, so the write in pass 3 is the final output) - Total traffic: 3*R + 2*W ≈ 5 passes Actually, this is fine. The roofline model accounts for the fact that softmax requires multiple passes. The bytes_formula in problem.yaml is just `2 * batch * vocab * 4` (read input + write output), which is the theoretical minimum. Real implementations will do more traffic than this. Let me proceed with the Triton implementation. Let me also think about whether I should do a fused max+sum online approach to reduce to 2 total passes. Actually, let me reconsider the "online softmax" approach more carefully for Triton. In the online algorithm, for each block: 1. Compute local max 2. Update global max: new_max = max(old_max, local_max) 3. Scale running sum: sum = sum * exp(old_max - new_max) 4. Compute local exp(x - new_max) and sum 5. Add local sum to running sum After processing all blocks: - We have the final max and sum - But we haven't written any output yet So we still need a second pass to write the output: y = exp(x - max) / sum. This gives us 2 passes over X (online pass + output pass), which is the same as my 3-pass approach above (pass 1 reads X, pass 2 reads X, pass 3 reads/writes Y). Hmm, but in my 3-pass approach: - Pass 1: read X (find max) - Pass 2: read X, write Y (exp + sum) - Pass 3: read Y, write Y (normalize) With online approach: - Pass 1: read X (online max + sum) - Pass 2: read X, write Y (exp + normalize) So the online approach saves the pass-3 read/write of Y! Total traffic: - Online: 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y = 3 passes - 3-pass: 2 reads of X + 1 read + 2 writes of Y = 5 passes The online approach is clearly better for memory bandwidth. Let me use that. But the challenge with online softmax is numerical accuracy. The correction factor `exp(old_max - new_max)` can lose precision when the difference is small. Let me think... Actually, the correction factor is fine because: - old_max and new_max are both fp32 values - When old_max == new_max (no update), the factor is exp(0) = 1.0 exactly - When old_max < new_max (update), we scale down the sum by exp(old_max - new_max) < 1.0 The issue is that exp(old_max - new_max) when (new_max - old_max) is small: say delta = 1e-6, then exp(-1e-6) ≈ 1 - 1e-6, and multiplying a large sum by this loses precision in the sum. But this is the same issue as in the standard approach. For Kahan summation in the online approach, we need to be careful because the Kahan compensation also needs to be scaled when the max updates. Let me think... **Online softmax with Kahan:** ``` max_val = -inf sum_val = 0.0 comp = 0.0 # Kahan compensation for block in blocks: local_max = max(x[block]) new_max = max(max_val, local_max) # Scale sum and compensation if new_max != max_val: correction = exp(max_val - new_max) sum_val = sum_val * correction comp = comp * correction # Scale compensation too max_val = new_max # Compute exp(x - max_val) and local sum exp_block = exp(x[block] - max_val) local_sum = sum(exp_block) # Triton tree reduction # Kahan accumulation y = local_sum - comp t = sum_val + y comp = (t - sum_val) - y sum_val = t # After all blocks: normalize inv_sum = 1.0 / sum_val # Second pass: write output for block in blocks: y[block] = exp(x[block] - max_val) * inv_sum ``` This should work and is numerically stable with Kahan. When we scale the compensation: ``` comp = comp * correction ``` This should work because the compensation represents the accumulated error in the sum, and when we scale the sum by `correction`, the error should also scale by `correction`. But there's a precision issue: if correction is close to 1.0 (i.e., the max barely changes), then `sum_val * correction` loses precision because it's essentially a no-op multiplication that can flip bits. Let me now write the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row_idx = pid if row_idx >= batch: return x_off = row_idx * stride_xb y_off = row_idx * stride_yb # --- Pass 1: Find row max --- row_max = -float('inf') cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) for start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offs = start + cols mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_off + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=-float('inf')) row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, tl.max(x)) # --- Pass 2: exp(x - max) + Kahan sum --- row_sum = 0.0 comp = 0.0 # Kahan compensation for start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offs = start + cols mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_off + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=0.0) e = tl.exp(x - row_max) tl.store(Y_ptr + y_off + offs * stride_yv, e, mask=mask) block_sum = tl.sum(e) # Kahan accumulation y = block_sum - comp t = row_sum + y comp = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t # --- Pass 3: Normalize --- inv_sum = 1.0 / row_sum for start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offs = start + cols mask = offs < vocab e = tl.load(Y_ptr + y_off + offs * stride_yv, mask=mask, other=0.0) tl.store(Y_ptr + y_off + offs * stride_yv, e * inv_sum, mask=mask) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch: int, vocab: int): super().__init__() self.batch = batch self.vocab = vocab def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: y = torch.empty_like(x) BLOCK_SIZE = 2048 grid = (self.batch,) softmax_kernel[grid]( x, y, self.batch, self.vocab, x.stride(0), x.stride(1), y.stride(0), y.stride(1), BLOCK_SIZE=BLOCK_SIZE, ) return y BATCH = 8 VOCAB = 32768 def get_inputs(): x = torch.randn(BATCH, VOCAB, dtype=torch.float32) * 4.0 return [x] def get_init_inputs(): return [BATCH, VOCAB] ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the Kahan compensation in Triton. The variables `row_sum`, `comp` are scalar values in the kernel (one per program). In Triton, these are just regular scalar variables that the compiler handles. The Kahan accumulation should work correctly. But there's a subtlety: `block_sum = tl.sum(e)` performs a tree reduction across the SIMD lanes. For BLOCK_SIZE=2048, this reduces 2048 fp32 values to one fp32 value. The tree reduction itself can lose precision, but for 2048 elements, the error should be small (a few ULPs). Then we accumulate these block sums across ~128 blocks (for 256K vocab) using Kahan. This should give us excellent numerical accuracy. Let me also think about the `tl.sum` precision. Triton's `tl.sum` in fp32 should be fine for 2048 elements. The tree reduction adds log2(2048) = 11 values, each of which can lose at most 1 ULP. So the block sum has at most ~11 ULPs of error, which is ~11 * 2^-23 ≈ 1.3e-6. This is well within our tolerance. Actually, `tl.sum` in Triton accumulates in the same dtype as the input (fp32 in our case). The intermediate sums in the tree reduction are all fp32. For 2048 positive values (exp values are always positive), the sum is at most 2048 * max_exp_val. The relative error of fp32 addition is at most 2^-24 per addition. With 11 levels of tree reduction, the relative error is at most 11 * 2^-24 ≈ 6.5e-7. This is fine. Now let me think about performance. The 3-pass approach reads the input twice and the output twice (write in pass 2, read+write in pass 3). But that's inherent to the algorithm. Hmm wait, let me re-read the roofline scoring. The bytes_formula is `"batch * vocab * 4 + batch * vocab * 4"` which is 2 * batch * vocab * 4 (read + write). And the achieved gbps is computed from this formula and the measured time. So if my kernel takes X ms, the achieved gbps is `(2 * batch * vocab * 4) / (X * 1e6)` GB/s. This means the roofline score doesn't penalize me for doing extra passes! It just measures the "useful" data movement and divides by time. So the more time I spend doing extra passes, the lower my effective bandwidth appears. With the 3-pass approach doing 5/2 times the theoretical minimum traffic, my peak fraction is capped at 2/5 = 40% even with perfect bandwidth utilization. Can I do better? Let me implement the online approach. **Online softmax with Kahan summation:** ```python @triton.jit def softmax_kernel_online( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row_idx = pid if row_idx >= batch: return x_off = row_idx * stride_xb y_off = row_idx * stride_yb cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) # Online softmax: single pass for max and sum row_max = -float('inf') row_sum = 0.0 comp = 0.0 # Kahan compensation for start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offs = start + cols mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_off + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=-float('inf')) # Update max block_max = tl.max(x) old_max = row_max row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, block_max) # Scale running sum and compensation correction = tl.exp(old_max - row_max) # This is 1.0 if row_max didn't change row_sum = row_sum * correction comp = comp * correction # Compute exp and accumulate sum e = tl.exp(x - row_max) block_sum = tl.sum(e) # Kahan accumulation y = block_sum - comp t = row_sum + y comp = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t # Second pass: write output inv_sum = 1.0 / row_sum for start in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): offs = start + cols mask = offs < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_off + offs * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=0.0) y_val = tl.exp(x - row_max) * inv_sum tl.store(Y_ptr + y_off + offs * stride_yv, y_val, mask=mask) ``` This does 2 reads of X and 1 write of Y. Let me also think about a potential issue with the online approach: numerical stability of the Kahan compensation when scaled. When `correction = exp(old_max - new_max)` is very close to 1.0 (say 1.0 - 1e-7), multiplying `row_sum` by `correction` gives `row_sum * (1 - 1e-7) ≈ row_sum - row_sum * 1e-7`. If `row_sum` is large (say 1000), then the error introduced is ~1e-4, which is significant. But the Kahan compensation should capture this error. Wait, the Kahan compensation is also being scaled: `comp = comp * correction`. If `correction` is close to 1.0, the scaling of `comp` is also approximately correct. But the error from the multiplication itself (`row_sum * correction`) might not be captured by the Kahan compensation because the Kahan accumulation only tracks the error from addition, not multiplication. Hmm, this is a subtle point. Let me think... The Kahan algorithm is designed for a sequence of additions: `s = ((...((a1 + a2) + a3) + ...) + an)`. It captures the rounding error from each addition. In our case, we're doing: 1. Scale: `row_sum *= correction`, `comp *= correction` 2. Add: `row_sum += block_sum` (with Kahan) The scaling step introduces a rounding error that the Kahan algorithm doesn't track. This error is bounded by `|row_sum| * |correction - 1| * eps_mach + |row_sum * correction| * eps_mach`. For correction close to 1.0, this is approximately `|row_sum| * |correction - 1| * eps_mach + |row_sum| * eps_mach`. The second term (`|row_sum| * eps_mach`) is the normal rounding error from multiplication, which is unavoidable. The first term is additional error from the scaling. For our problem, the max typically doesn't change much after the first few blocks (for the normal distribution). So `correction` is 1.0 for most blocks, and the scaling error is 0. For the few blocks where the max does change, the correction factor is `exp(old_max - new_max)`, which could be significantly less than 1. Wait, that's not right. Let me think more carefully. For fp32 addition, each addition has a relative error of eps_mach ≈ 1.2e-7. But the absolute error of adding a small number `a` to a large number `s` is approximately `s * eps_mach`, not `a * eps_mach`. So for summing exp values: - After processing the "large" exp values (say 100 values near 1.0), the sum is ~100. - Adding a "small" exp value (say 1e-10) to a sum of 100: - Exact: 100.0000000001 - fp32: 100.0 (the small value is lost because 1e-10 < 100 * eps_mach ≈ 1.2e-5) - But losing a 1e-10 value is fine because the final result for that element is 1e-10 / sum ≈ 1e-12, and atol=1e-5. Here's my final kernel design: ```python @triton.jit def softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid if row >= batch: return x_base = row * stride_xb y_base = row * stride_yb offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE) # ---- Online pass: compute row max and row sum with Kahan ---- row_max = float('-inf') row_sum = 0.0 kah_c = 0.0 for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): c = s + offs mask = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_base + c * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=float('-inf')) # Update running max bmax = tl.max(x) old_max = row_max row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, bmax) # Rescale sum and Kahan compensation corr = tl.exp(old_max - row_max) row_sum *= corr kah_c *= corr # Local exp + sum e = tl.exp(x - row_max) bsum = tl.sum(e) # Kahan accumulate y = bsum - kah_c t = row_sum + y kah_c = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t # ---- Output pass: exp(x - max) / sum ---- inv_sum = 1.0 / row_sum for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK_SIZE): c = s + offs mask = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + x_base + c * stride_xv, mask=mask, other=0.0) y = tl.exp(x - row_max) * inv_sum tl.store(Y_ptr + y_base + c * stride_yv, y, mask=mask) ``` Wait, I need to think about `tl.exp(old_max - row_max)` when `old_max = -inf` and `row_max = -inf` (which happens for the very first iteration if all values are -inf, which shouldn't happen in practice). In that case, `exp(-inf - (-inf)) = exp(NaN)` = NaN. But this only happens if the row is all -inf, which is not a valid input. Actually, let me handle this edge case. In the first block: - `old_max = -inf`, `bmax = some_finite_value` - `row_max = max(-inf, bmax) = bmax` - `corr = exp(-inf - bmax) = exp(-inf) = 0` - `row_sum = 0 * 0 = 0` (was already 0) - `kah_c = 0 * 0 = 0` - Then we compute exp(x - bmax) and sum, which gives the correct result. OK, the `-inf - (-inf)` case doesn't happen because after the first block, `row_max` is updated to a finite value (assuming the input has at least one finite value per row). Also, I should make sure the kernel handles the case where `vocab = 0` (empty tensor). But the shapes don't include this case, so I won't worry about it. OK, writing the code now. Here's the complete solution: ```python """Numerically tight softmax kernel using Triton with Kahan summation. Implements online softmax (max + sum in one pass) with Kahan-compensated summation for numerical accuracy on long reductions (up to 256K vocab). Second pass writes normalized output. """ import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def _softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): row = tl.program_id(0) if row >= batch: return xb = row * stride_xb yb = row * stride_yb cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) # --- Online pass: running max + Kahan-compensated sum --- rmax = -float('inf') rsum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 # Kahan compensation for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK): c = s + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float('inf')) bmax = tl.max(x) old = rmax rmax = tl.maximum(rmax, bmax) # rescale running sum and compensation corr = tl.exp(old - rmax) rsum *= corr kc *= corr e = tl.exp(x - rmax) bsum = tl.sum(e) # Kahan accumulate y = bsum - kc t = rsum + y kc = (t - rsum) - y rsum = t # --- Output pass --- inv = 1.0 / rsum for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK): c = s + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) tl.store(Y_ptr + yb + c * stride_yv, tl.exp(x - rmax) * inv, mask=m) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch: int, vocab: int): super().__init__() self.batch = batch self.vocab = vocab def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: y = torch.empty_like(x) BLOCK = 2048 _softmax_kernel[(self.batch,)]( x, y, self.batch, self.vocab, x.stride(0), x.stride(1), y.stride(0), y.stride(1), BLOCK=BLOCK, num_warps=8, ) return y BATCH = 8 VOCAB = 32768 def get_inputs(): return [torch.randn(BATCH, VOCAB, dtype=torch.float32) * 4.0] def get_init_inputs(): return [BATCH, VOCAB] ``` This looks good. So total traffic is: - Online pass: read X (full) - Output pass: read X (full) + write Y (full) - Total: 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y = 3 * n * 4 bytes The theoretical minimum is 1 read of X + 1 write of Y = 2 * n * 4 bytes. So my kernel does 1.5x the minimum memory traffic. The peak fraction is capped at 2/3 = 67% of peak bandwidth. Can I do better? The absolute minimum for numerically stable softmax is: - 1 read of X (for max) - 1 read of X (for exp and sum) - 1 write of Y (output) - That's 2 reads + 1 write = 3 * n * 4 bytes Unless I store the exp values from the max-finding pass, I always need 2 reads of X. But wait, the exp values stored to scratch might be wrong if the max changes later! """SOTA reference for last-dim softmax.

Preference order:
  1. liger-kernel's Triton softmax (LigerSoftmaxFunction) — fused, fp32-acc
  2. torch.compile(torch.softmax) — Inductor generates a Triton kernel that
     is competitive on bandwidth-bound reductions

Agents are FORBIDDEN from importing either of these in solution.py (see
problem.yaml.forbidden). This file is only the benchmark's reference line.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import torch

_compiled_softmax = None


def _liger_softmax(x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor | None:
    try:
        from liger_kernel.ops.softmax import LigerSoftmaxFunction
        return LigerSoftmaxFunction.apply(x)
    except Exception:
        return None


def _compiled(x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
    global _compiled_softmax
    if _compiled_softmax is None:
        _compiled_softmax = torch.compile(
            lambda t: torch.softmax(t, dim=-1),
            mode="reduce-overhead",
        )
    return _compiled_softmax(x)


def sota_forward(x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
    """Best-available softmax reference. x: (batch, vocab) fp32."""
    out = _liger_softmax(x)
    if out is not None:
        return out
    return _compiled(x)


def is_available() -> bool:
    return True  # torch.compile fallback is always available

+"""Numerically tight softmax via Triton with online Kahan summation.
+
+Two-pass approach:
+  Pass 1 (online): single scan over the row to find the running max and a
+                   Kahan-compensated sum of exp(x - running_max).
+  Pass 2:          re-read x, write exp(x - final_max) / final_sum.
+
+Total DRAM traffic: 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y = 3 * N * 4 bytes,
+which is optimal for numerically stable softmax (must see the whole row
+before the max is known, then must write normalized output).
+"""
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _softmax_kernel(
+    X_ptr, Y_ptr,
+    batch, vocab,
+    stride_xb, stride_xv,
+    stride_yb, stride_yv,
+    BLOCK: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    row = tl.program_id(0)
+    if row >= batch:
+        return
+
+    xb = row * stride_xb
+    yb = row * stride_yb
+    cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK)
+
+    # ---- Online pass: running max + Kahan-compensated sum ----
+    rmax = -float("inf")
+    rsum = 0.0
+    kc = 0.0  # Kahan compensation
+
+    for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK):
+        c = s + cols
+        m = c < vocab
+        x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf"))
+
+        bmax = tl.max(x)
+        old = rmax
+        rmax = tl.maximum(rmax, bmax)
+
+        # Rescale running sum and compensation when max increases
+        corr = tl.exp(old - rmax)
+        rsum = rsum * corr
+        kc = kc * corr
+
+        e = tl.exp(x - rmax)
+        bsum = tl.sum(e)
+
+        # Kahan accumulate block sum into row sum
+        y = bsum - kc
+        t = rsum + y
+        kc = (t - rsum) - y
+        rsum = t
+
+    # ---- Output pass: exp(x - max) * inv_sum ----
+    inv = 1.0 / rsum
+    for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK):
+        c = s + cols
+        m = c < vocab
+        x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0)
+        tl.store(Y_ptr + yb + c * stride_yv, tl.exp(x - rmax) * inv, mask=m)
+
+
+class Model(nn.Module):
+    def __init__(self, batch: int, vocab: int):
+        super().__init__()
+        self.batch = batch
+        self.vocab = vocab
+
+    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
+        y = torch.empty_like(x)
+        grid = (self.batch,)
+        _softmax_kernel[grid](
+            x, y,
+            self.batch, self.vocab,
+            x.stride(0), x.stride(1),
+            y.stride(0), y.stride(1),
+            BLOCK=2048,
+            num_warps=8,
+        )
+        return y
+
+
+BATCH = 8
+VOCAB = 32768
+
+
+def get_inputs():
+    return [torch.randn(BATCH, VOCAB, dtype=torch.float32) * 4.0]
+
+
+def get_init_inputs():
+    return [BATCH, VOCAB]

reasoning (66546 chars)
The correctness passes but performance is low. The peak_fraction is 0.0601 (6%), which is far below the 10% threshold for OK. Let me analyze the results: Shape 0 (batch=32, vocab=4096): solution = 83.9 GB/s, eager = 15.6 GB/s Shape 1 (batch=16, vocab=32768): solution = 173.6 GB/s, eager = 67.7 GB/s Shape 2 (batch=8, vocab=131072): solution = 132.3 GB/s, eager = 116.0 GB/s Shape 3 (batch=4, vocab=262144): solution = 73.2 GB/s, eager = 115.3 GB/s Shape 4 (batch=8, vocab=131072, extreme): solution = 105.2 GB/s, eager = 116.6 GB/s The problem is: 1. For small shapes (0, 1), the kernel is faster than eager because the data fits in cache and there's enough parallelism (32 and 16 rows). 2. For larger shapes (2-4), the kernel is slower than eager! With only 4-8 rows, we have 4-8 programs, which severely underutilizes the GPU. The key issue is **GPU utilization**. With 4-8 programs on a GPU with ~170 SMs, most SMs are idle. To improve performance, I need to increase parallelism. Options: 1. **Split rows across multiple programs**: Each program handles a chunk of a row. Use separate kernels for max-reduction and output. 2. **Process multiple rows per program**: Launch more programs, each handling a subset of elements across multiple rows. 3. **Use 2D grid**: Programs indexed by (row, col_block). Let me think about option 1 in more detail: **Multi-kernel approach:** 1. Kernel 1 (row_block_max): Each program processes one block of one row. Compute local max. Write to scratch[batch, num_blocks]. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) - num_blocks = ceil(vocab / BLOCK) 2. Kernel 2 (row_max): Each program processes one row. Reduce scratch to get row max. Write to max_buf[batch]. - Grid: (batch,) 3. Kernel 3 (exp_sum): Each program processes one block of one row. Compute exp(x - max) and local sum. Write exp values to Y, sum to scratch[batch, num_blocks]. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) 4. Kernel 4 (row_sum): Each program processes one row. Reduce scratch to get row sum. Write to sum_buf[batch]. - Grid: (batch,) 5. Kernel 5 (normalize): Each program processes one block of one row. Read Y, divide by sum, write back. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) This is 5 kernel launches. The benefit is that kernels 1, 3, and 5 launch `batch * num_blocks` programs, which gives much better GPU utilization. For batch=4, vocab=262144, BLOCK=2048: num_blocks = 128. So kernels 1, 3, 5 launch 512 programs. That's much better than 4 programs. But 5 kernel launches is a lot of overhead, especially for small shapes. Let me see if I can reduce the number of kernels. **Reduced multi-kernel approach:** 1. Kernel 1 (row_max): One program per row, loops over blocks to find max. Write max to max_buf[batch]. - Grid: (batch,) - This is the same as my current approach's pass 1, just for max. 2. Kernel 2 (exp_sum_normalize): Each program processes one block of one row. Compute exp(x - max), accumulate local sum. Write exp values to Y. Also write local sum to scratch. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) 3. Kernel 3 (row_sum_normalize): One program per row. Reduce scratch to get row sum. Compute inv_sum. Then loop over Y and normalize. - Grid: (batch,) Or even better, I can do the sum reduction across programs using atomic adds: 1. Kernel 1 (max): One program per row, loops over blocks to find max. - Grid: (batch,) 2. Kernel 2 (exp_sum): Each program processes one block of one row. Compute exp(x - max), write to Y, atomic-add local sum to sum_buf[row]. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) 3. Kernel 3 (normalize): Each program processes one block of one row. Read Y, divide by sum_buf[row], write back. - Grid: (batch, num_blocks) This is 3 kernel launches with good parallelism in kernels 2 and 3. But atomic adds in fp32 might be slow. Let me check if Triton supports atomic add for fp32. In Triton, `tl.atomic_add(ptr, val)` supports fp32. On NVIDIA GPUs, this maps to `atomicAdd` which is supported for fp32. However, atomic adds can be slow due to contention. With 128 programs all doing atomic add to sum_buf[row], there's significant contention on 4 memory locations. A better approach: use a scratch buffer for local sums and reduce in a separate kernel or in the normalization kernel. Let me try the 3-kernel approach: ```python # Kernel 1: find row max (one program per row, loops over blocks) @triton.jit def _max_kernel(X_ptr, max_buf, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): row = tl.program_id(0) if row >= batch: return xb = row * stride_xb cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) rmax = -float("inf") for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK): c = s + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf")) rmax = tl.maximum(rmax, tl.max(x)) tl.store(max_buf + row, rmax) # Kernel 2: exp(x - max) + write to Y + accumulate sum to scratch @triton.jit def _exp_sum_kernel(X_ptr, Y_ptr, max_buf, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, NUM_BLOCKS: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // NUM_BLOCKS blk = pid % NUM_BLOCKS if row >= batch: return rmax = tl.load(max_buf + row) start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab xb = row * stride_xb yb = row * stride_yv # Wait, this should be stride_yb x = tl.load(X_ptr + xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) e = tl.exp(x - rmax) tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, e, mask=m) bsum = tl.sum(e) tl.store(sum_scratch + pid, bsum) # Store block sum to scratch # Kernel 3: reduce sums + normalize @triton.jit def _normalize_kernel(Y_ptr, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, NUM_BLOCKS: tl.constexpr): row = tl.program_id(0) if row >= batch: return # Reduce sum_scratch[row * NUM_BLOCKS : (row+1) * NUM_BLOCKS] rsum = 0.0 for i in range(NUM_BLOCKS): rsum += tl.load(sum_scratch + row * NUM_BLOCKS + i) inv = 1.0 / rsum yb = row * stride_yb cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) for s in range(0, vocab, BLOCK): c = s + cols m = c < vocab y = tl.load(Y_ptr + yb + c * stride_yv, mask=m, other=0.0) tl.store(Y_ptr + yb + c * stride_yv, y * inv, mask=m) ``` Wait, this is getting complicated. Let me think about a simpler approach. Actually, the main bottleneck is the number of programs. With only 4-8 programs for the large shapes, we're severely underutilizing the GPU. A simpler optimization: **fuse the online pass and output into a single kernel, but launch more programs.** I can split each row into segments, where each segment is processed by a separate program. The first segment of each row computes the max and sum (online), then broadcasts them to the other segments. But this requires synchronization between programs, which Triton doesn't support well. Alternative: **Use a two-kernel approach where the first kernel computes per-row max and sum (with good parallelism), and the second kernel computes the output.** **Kernel 1: Per-row max and sum, using a 2D grid** Actually, let me try a different approach. Instead of the online approach, I'll use: 1. Kernel 1: Each program handles one block of one row. Compute local max and local sum. Write to scratch. - Grid: (batch, num_col_blocks) → flat grid of batch * num_col_blocks - Scratch: local_max[batch][num_col_blocks], local_sum[batch][num_col_blocks] 2. Kernel 2: Each program handles one row. Reduce local_max and local_sum across blocks. - Grid: (batch,) - Output: row_max[batch], row_sum[batch] 3. Kernel 3: Each program handles one block of one row. Compute exp(x - max) / sum and write to Y. - Grid: (batch, num_col_blocks) → flat grid of batch * num_col_blocks Wait, but kernel 1 can't compute local_sum because the sum depends on the max, and the max is not known until after kernel 2. So I need: 1. Kernel 1: Compute local max per block. 2. Kernel 2: Reduce to global max per row. 3. Kernel 3: Compute exp(x - global_max) per block, accumulate local sum. 4. Kernel 4: Reduce to global sum per row (with Kahan). 5. Kernel 5: Normalize. That's 5 kernels. Too many. Let me simplify. The key insight is that for the max reduction, I can do it in a single kernel (one program per row, looping over blocks). This is fast because: - Finding max is a single pass over the data - With only 4-8 rows, 4-8 programs are launched - Each program streams through 1-4 MB of data, which is enough to saturate a few SMs The bottleneck is the output pass, where we need to normalize. Let me keep the max computation as a single program per row, but parallelize the exp+sum and normalize passes. **Revised plan:** 1. Kernel A (max): One program per row. Loop over blocks, find max. Store to max_buf[batch]. - Grid: (batch,) - Traffic: 1 read of X per row 2. Kernel B (exp + sum): One program per (row, col_block). Compute exp(x - max), write to Y, store local sum to scratch. - Grid: (batch * ceil(vocab / BLOCK),) - Traffic: 1 read of X + 1 write of Y + tiny write (scratch) per block 3. Kernel C (reduce + normalize): One program per row. Reduce scratch to get row sum. Then loop over Y to normalize. - Grid: (batch,) - Traffic: tiny read (scratch) + 1 read of Y + 1 write of Y per row Total traffic: - Kernel A: 1 read of X - Kernel B: 1 read of X + 1 write of Y - Kernel C: 1 read of Y + 1 write of Y - Total: 2 reads of X + 2 reads of Y + 2 writes of Y? No wait, Y is written in B and read+written in C. - Total bytes: 2 * N * 4 (X reads) + 1 * N * 4 (Y write in B) + 1 * N * 4 (Y read in C) + 1 * N * 4 (Y write in C) = 5 * N * 4 Hmm, that's 5 passes of traffic, more than the online approach's 3 passes. The normalization pass reads Y back from DRAM. But the key benefit is that kernels B (and potentially C with parallelization) have much better GPU utilization. Let me think about this trade-off. For large shapes: - Online approach (1 kernel, 3 passes): Low utilization but less traffic - Multi-kernel approach (3 kernels, 5 passes): High utilization but more traffic Which is better depends on the actual numbers. Let me estimate. For batch=4, vocab=262144: - Data size: 4 * 262144 * 4 = 4 MB per array - Peak bandwidth: 1800 GB/s Online approach: - Traffic: 3 * 4 MB = 12 MB - With 4 programs, maybe 10% of peak = 180 GB/s - Time: 12 MB / 180 GB/s = 0.067 ms Multi-kernel approach: - Traffic: 5 * 4 MB = 20 MB - Kernel B has 512 programs, maybe 80% of peak = 1440 GB/s - But kernels A and C have 4 programs, maybe 10% = 180 GB/s - Kernel A time: 4 MB / 180 GB/s = 0.022 ms - Kernel B time: 8 MB / 1440 GB/s = 0.006 ms - Kernel C time: 8 MB / 180 GB/s = 0.044 ms - Total: 0.072 ms Hmm, the multi-kernel approach is actually slower due to kernels A and C being bottlenecks, plus kernel launch overhead. The real issue is that the normalization pass (kernel C) reads and writes Y, and with only 4 programs, it's slow. Can I parallelize kernel C too? **Fully parallelized approach:** 1. Kernel A (max): One program per row. Find max. Store to max_buf. - Grid: (batch,) - 4 programs, 4 MB read 2. Kernel B (exp): One program per (row, col_block). Compute exp(x - max), write to Y, store local sum to scratch. - Grid: (batch * ceil(vocab / BLOCK),) - 512 programs, 4 MB read + 4 MB write 3. Kernel C (sum_reduce): One program per row. Reduce scratch. Store row_sum. - Grid: (batch,) - 4 programs, tiny read (128 fp32 values per row = 512 bytes) 4. Kernel D (normalize): One program per (row, col_block). Read Y, divide by row_sum, write back. - Grid: (batch * ceil(vocab / BLOCK),) - 512 programs, 4 MB read + 4 MB write Total traffic: - Kernel A: 4 MB read - Kernel B: 4 MB read + 4 MB write - Kernel C: ~2 KB read - Kernel D: 4 MB read + 4 MB write - Total: 12 MB read + 8 MB write = 20 MB But with good utilization on kernels B and D (512 programs each), the time should be: - Kernel A: ~0.022 ms - Kernel B: 8 MB / 1440 GB/s ≈ 0.006 ms - Kernel C: ~negligible - Kernel D: 8 MB / 1440 GB/s ≈ 0.006 ms - Total: ~0.034 ms But with kernel launch overhead (~5-10 μs per kernel), total is ~0.034 + 4 * 0.007 ≈ 0.062 ms. The "useful" bandwidth is 2 * 4 MB / 0.062 ms = 8 MB / 0.062 ms ≈ 129 GB/s, or 7.2% of peak. Hmm, that's similar to what I'm getting now. The issue is that even with parallel kernels B and D, the serial parts (kernel A, C) and kernel launch overhead dominate. Let me think about a different approach entirely. What if I use **cooperative kernel launches** where thread blocks cooperate via shared memory? In CUDA, cooperative groups allow thread blocks to synchronize with each other. This would let me launch many thread blocks for the same row and have them cooperate on the reduction. But Triton doesn't support cooperative groups. I'd need to write this in CUDA C++. Alternatively, I can use a **single-kernel approach with atomics**: ```python @triton.jit def _softmax_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, max_buf, sum_buf, batch, vocab, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) num_col_blocks = tl.cdiv(vocab, BLOCK) row = pid // num_col_blocks blk = pid % num_col_blocks if row >= batch: return start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab # Load block of x x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf")) # Local max local_max = tl.max(x, axis=0) # Atomic max to get row max # Triton doesn't have atomicMax for fp32 directly, but we can use a CAS loop # Or we can convert to int and use atomicMax on ints ... ``` Actually, Triton doesn't have `tl.atomic_max` for fp32. It has `tl.atomic_add` for fp32 and `tl.atomic_max` for integers. I'd need to bitcast fp32 to int32, use atomic_max, and bitcast back. But this is fragile. Let me try a different approach. What about using CUDA C++ via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`? This gives me full control over the kernel. Actually, let me first try to improve the Triton kernel with a different strategy. Instead of one program per row, I can: 1. Use a 1D grid with `batch * num_col_blocks` programs 2. First, each program computes local max and writes to scratch 3. Then, use a barrier or separate kernel to reduce 4. Then, each program reads global max, computes exp, writes to Y, and atomic-adds to sum 5. Barrier or separate kernel for sum reduction 6. Each program reads global sum, normalizes its block of Y The problem is that Triton doesn't have inter-program barriers. So I'd need multiple kernel launches. Let me try the multi-kernel approach with just 2 additional kernels (reducing the number from 4 to 3): 1. **Kernel 1 (block_max)**: Launch `batch * num_blocks` programs. Each computes local max and writes to `max_scratch[row * num_blocks + blk]`. 2. **Kernel 2 (softmax)**: Launch `batch * num_blocks` programs. Each reads row max (reduced from max_scratch by looping in-program), computes exp(x - max), writes to Y, and writes local sum to `sum_scratch[row * num_blocks + blk]`. 3. **Kernel 3 (normalize)**: Launch `batch * num_blocks` programs. Each reads row sum (reduced from sum_scratch), reads Y, divides by sum, writes back. Wait, kernel 2 needs the row max, which requires reducing max_scratch. This is O(num_blocks) extra work per program, but num_blocks is small (128 for 256K vocab with BLOCK=2048). Reading 128 fp32 values (512 bytes) is negligible compared to reading 2048 fp32 values (8KB) from X. This gives 3 kernels: 1. block_max: `batch * num_blocks` programs, writes max_scratch 2. softmax: `batch * num_blocks` programs, reads max_scratch + X, writes Y + sum_scratch 3. normalize: `batch * num_blocks` programs, reads sum_scratch + Y, writes Y For batch=4, vocab=262144, BLOCK=2048, num_blocks=128: - Each kernel launches 512 programs - Good utilization! Maybe the issue isn't the number of programs but the number of warps. With 8 warps per program and 4 programs, I have 32 warps. Actually, let me just try the multi-kernel approach directly. It should give a much bigger improvement. Wait, let me think about the sum reduction. In kernel 3, each program needs the row sum. It reads `num_blocks` values from sum_scratch and sums them. For 128 blocks, that's a loop of 128 iterations reading fp32 values. But the values are from global memory and are contiguous in memory. With 512 programs all reading from the same scratch area, there might be cache contention. Actually, each row has its own sum_scratch values, so 4 rows × 128 values = 512 values = 2 KB. Each block sum is already an fp32 sum of ~2048 exp values. The sum reduction across ~128 block sums is just adding ~128 fp32 values. With 128 additions in fp32, the accumulated error is ~128 * eps_mach ≈ 128 * 1.2e-7 ≈ 1.5e-5. That's right at the tolerance! So I might need Kahan for the cross-block sum reduction too. For positive values, the tree reduction (or sequential sum) is actually quite accurate because there's no cancellation. The relative error per addition is at most eps_mach, and with 128 additions, the total relative error is at most 128 * eps_mach ≈ 1.5e-5. But this is worst-case. In practice, the error is much smaller because most block sums are much smaller than the total sum. So the relative error of the total is dominated by the error in the largest block sums. Let me just include Kahan to be safe. It's minimal overhead. OK, let me implement the multi-kernel approach. Here's the plan: ```python @triton.jit def _block_max_kernel(X_ptr, max_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf")) local_max = tl.max(x) tl.store(max_scratch + pid, local_max) @triton.jit def _exp_sum_kernel(X_ptr, Y_ptr, max_scratch, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # Reduce max_scratch for this row row_max = -float("inf") for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(max_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, v) start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) e = tl.exp(x - row_max) tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, e, mask=m) local_sum = tl.sum(e) tl.store(sum_scratch + pid, local_sum) @triton.jit def _normalize_kernel(Y_ptr, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # Reduce sum_scratch for this row with Kahan row_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(sum_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) y = v - kc t = row_sum + y kc = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t inv = 1.0 / row_sum start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab y = tl.load(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, mask=m, other=0.0) tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, y * inv, mask=m) ``` Wait, the normalize kernel still reads and writes Y, which is 2 passes of Y. Let me check if I can avoid this by fusing the exp and normalize in a single kernel. Actually, the issue is that we need the row sum before we can normalize. The row sum depends on all blocks' sums. So we need: 1. All blocks compute their local sums (kernel 2) 2. Reduce to get row sum 3. Normalize (kernel 3) Can I fuse steps 2 and 3? In kernel 3, I first reduce sum_scratch to get the row sum, then normalize my block of Y. This is fine - the reduction is O(num_blocks) per program, and the normalization is O(BLOCK) per program. But the problem is that kernel 3 reads Y from DRAM (written by kernel 2) and writes it back. This is 2 passes of Y in DRAM. Can I avoid this by having kernel 2 not write exp values to Y, and instead have kernel 3 compute exp and normalize in one shot? ```python @triton.jit def _exp_sum_kernel(X_ptr, sum_scratch, max_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # Reduce max_scratch for this row row_max = -float("inf") for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(max_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, v) start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) e = tl.exp(x - row_max) local_sum = tl.sum(e) tl.store(sum_scratch + pid, local_sum) @triton.jit def _output_kernel(X_ptr, Y_ptr, max_scratch, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # Reduce max_scratch row_max = -float("inf") for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(max_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) row_max = tl.maximum(row_max, v) # Reduce sum_scratch with Kahan row_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(sum_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) y = v - kc t = row_sum + y kc = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t inv = 1.0 / row_sum start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) y = tl.exp(x - row_max) * inv tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, y, mask=m) ``` This is better! Now: - Kernel 1 (block_max): Read X, write max_scratch. `batch * num_blocks` programs. - Kernel 2 (exp_sum): Read max_scratch + X, write sum_scratch. `batch * num_blocks` programs. - Kernel 3 (output): Read max_scratch + sum_scratch + X, write Y. `batch * num_blocks` programs. Traffic: - Kernel 1: 4 MB read (X) - Kernel 2: 512 B (max_scratch) + 4 MB read (X) - Kernel 3: 512 B (max_scratch) + 2 KB (sum_scratch) + 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) - Total: 12 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) + ~3 KB scratch That's 3 reads of X + 1 write of Y = 4 passes of data. Hmm, that's worse than the online approach's 2 reads + 1 write = 3 passes. But with much better GPU utilization! The key question is whether the improved utilization compensates for the extra traffic. Actually, can I fuse kernels 2 and 3? In kernel 2, I compute exp(x - max) and the local sum. If I also had the global sum, I could write the normalized output directly. But the global sum depends on all blocks' local sums, which haven't been computed yet. What if I use the approach from the original online kernel, but with more programs? Let me think... **Hybrid approach:** 1. Kernel 1 (max): One program per row, loop over blocks, find max. Store to max_buf[batch]. - Grid: (batch,) - This is fast because it's a single pass with sequential access. 2. Kernel 2 (output): One program per (row, col_block). Read max, compute exp(x - max), accumulate local sum to scratch. - Grid: (batch * num_blocks,) - Store exp values to Y (or keep in registers? Re-read X, compute exp(x - max) / sum, write to Y. - Grid: (batch * num_blocks,) Traffic: - Kernel 1: 4 MB read (X) - Kernel 2: 512 B (max_buf, shared across programs in same row... wait, max_buf is batch-sized) + 4 MB read (X) + 8 KB write (sum_scratch) - Kernel 3: 2 KB (sum_scratch) + 512 B (max_buf) + 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) - Total: 12 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) That's 3 reads of X + 1 write of Y per data array = 4 passes. The max_buf and sum_scratch are tiny. But actually, do I need kernel 2? I need the row sum before I can normalize. The row sum requires all blocks to have computed their local sums. If I launch all blocks in parallel, they can each compute their local sum and write to sum_scratch. Then kernel 3 reads sum_scratch, reduces, and writes output. But wait, kernel 2 doesn't need to write exp values to Y. It only needs to compute local sums and write to sum_scratch. So kernel 2 is just: ```python @triton.jit def _sum_kernel(X_ptr, max_buf, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return row_max = tl.load(max_buf + row) start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) e = tl.exp(x - row_max) local_sum = tl.sum(e) tl.store(sum_scratch + pid, local_sum) ``` And kernel 3 (output): ```python @triton.jit def _output_kernel(X_ptr, Y_ptr, max_buf, sum_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return row_max = tl.load(max_buf + row) # Kahan reduce sum_scratch row_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): v = tl.load(sum_scratch + row * num_blocks + i) y = v - kc t = row_sum + y kc = (t - row_sum) - y row_sum = t inv = 1.0 / row_sum start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) y = tl.exp(x - row_max) * inv tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, y, mask=m) ``` Traffic: - Kernel 1 (max): 4 MB read (X) + 16 B write (max_buf) - Kernel 2 (sum): 16 B read (max_buf) + 4 MB read (X) + 2 KB write (sum_scratch) - Kernel 3 (output): 16 B read (max_buf) + 2 KB read (sum_scratch) + 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) - Total: 12 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) + ~4 KB scratch "Useful" traffic: 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) = 8 MB. Actual traffic: 12 MB read + 4 MB write = 16 MB. Overhead: 16 / 8 = 2x. With good utilization on all 3 kernels (512 programs each): Peak bandwidth 1800 GB/s. Let's assume 80% utilization = 1440 GB/s. Kernel 1 (4 programs for batch=4): Maybe only 200 GB/s. - Time: 4 MB / 200 GB/s = 0.020 ms Kernel 2 (512 programs): 1440 GB/s. - Traffic: ~4 MB read - Time: 4 MB / 1440 GB/s = 0.003 ms Kernel 3 (512 programs): 1440 GB/s. - Traffic: ~8 MB (read X + write Y) - Time: 8 MB / 1440 GB/s = 0.006 ms Kernel launch overhead: 3 * 0.005 ms = 0.015 ms Total: 0.020 + 0.003 + 0.006 + 0.015 = 0.044 ms Useful bandwidth: 8 MB / 0.044 ms = 182 GB/s Peak fraction: 182 / 1800 = 10.1% Hmm, still only 10%. The bottleneck is kernel 1 with only 4 programs. Can I parallelize kernel 1 too? Yes, with the block_max approach: Kernel 1 (block_max): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Each finds local max. Write to max_scratch[batch * num_blocks]. But then I need a kernel 1b to reduce max_scratch to max_buf. That's another kernel launch. Or I can have kernel 2 reduce max_scratch internally (as in my earlier design). This avoids the extra kernel. Let me redesign: 1. Kernel 1 (block_max): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Find local max per block. Write to max_scratch. 2. Kernel 2 (sum): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Reduce max_scratch per row, compute exp + local sum. Write to sum_scratch. 3. Kernel 3 (output): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Reduce sum_scratch per row, read X, compute exp/max/sum. Write to Y. All kernels have `batch * num_blocks` programs = 512 programs for the largest shape. Traffic: - Kernel 1: 4 MB read (X) + 2 KB write (max_scratch) - Kernel 2: 2 KB read (max_scratch) + 4 MB read (X) + 2 KB write (sum_scratch) - Kernel 3: 2 KB read (sum_scratch) + 2 KB read (max_scratch) + 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) - Total: 12 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) + ~6 KB scratch Time at 1440 GB/s effective: - Kernel 1: 4 MB / 1440 = 0.003 ms - Kernel 2: 4 MB / 1440 = 0.003 ms - Kernel 3: 8 MB / 1440 = 0.006 ms - Launch overhead: 3 * 0.005 = 0.015 ms - Total: 0.027 ms Useful bandwidth: 8 MB / 0.027 ms = 296 GB/s Peak fraction: 296 / 1800 = 16.4% Better! But still low because of the 3x read overhead and kernel launch overhead. Actually, I can fuse kernels 1 and 2. If kernel 2 reduces max_scratch itself, then I need kernel 1 to write max_scratch first. But what if kernel 1 also computes the exp and local sum? That requires knowing the row max, which kernel 1 doesn't know yet. Hmm, this is the chicken-and-egg problem. To compute exp(x - max), I need the max. But the max requires seeing all blocks. What about the online approach with multiple programs? Each program computes a local online max and sum, then we need to merge them. For the online approach with multiple programs: - Each program processes one block and maintains a local (max, sum) pair - After all programs finish, we need to merge the (max, sum) pairs: - Global max = max of all local maxs - Global sum = sum of all local_sums * exp(local_max - global_max) This can be done in two kernels: 1. Kernel 1 (online_block): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Compute local (max, exp_sum) pair. Write to scratch. 2. Kernel 2 (merge + output): `batch * num_blocks` programs. Reduce scratch per row to get (global_max, global_sum). Read X, compute exp(x - global_max) / global_sum. Write to Y. Traffic: - Kernel 1: 4 MB read (X) + ~4 KB write (scratch: max + sum per block) - Kernel 2: ~4 KB read (scratch) + 4 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) - Total: 8 MB read (X) + 4 MB write (Y) + ~8 KB scratch This is only 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y! Same as the single-program online approach, but with much better utilization! Time at 1440 GB/s: - Kernel 1: 4 MB / 1440 = 0.003 ms - Kernel 2: 8 MB / 1440 = 0.006 ms - Launch overhead: 2 * 0.005 = 0.010 ms - Total: 0.019 ms Useful bandwidth: 8 MB / 0.019 ms = 421 GB/s Peak fraction: 421 / 1800 = 23.4% That's much better! And this is the same traffic as the single-program online approach (2 reads + 1 write). The key insight is to split the online approach into two kernel launches, where the first kernel computes per-block (max, sum) pairs, and the second kernel merges them and writes output. For the Kahan summation in the merge step: when merging (max_i, sum_i) pairs into a global (max, sum), the compensation for scaling is sum_i * exp(max_i - global_max). This is equivalent to the online approach's correction factor. For Kahan summation in the merge: ``` global_max = max of all max_i global_sum = sum of sum_i * exp(max_i - global_max) ``` The Kahan accumulation is: ``` global_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for each block i: corrected = sum_i * exp(max_i - global_max) y = corrected - kc t = global_sum + y kc = (t - global_sum) - y global_sum = t ``` This should give excellent numerical accuracy. Let me implement this approach! Actually, there's a subtlety. In the per-block online pass, each block computes: ``` local_max = max(x[block]) local_sum = sum(exp(x[block] - local_max)) ``` Then in the merge, the global sum is: ``` sum(exp(x[all] - global_max)) = sum_i sum(exp(x[block_i] - global_max)) = sum_i sum(exp(x[block_i] - local_max_i) * exp(local_max_i - global_max)) = sum_i local_sum_i * exp(local_max_i - global_max) ``` This is correct. Let me implement this. Actually wait, I need to double-check the merge step. Let me be more careful. For each block i: - local_max_i = max(x[block_i]) - local_sum_i = sum(exp(x[block_i] - local_max_i)) The global max is: - global_max = max_i(local_max_i) The global sum is: - global_sum = sum over all elements j of exp(x[j] - global_max) - = sum_i sum_{j in block_i} exp(x[j] - global_max) - = sum_i sum_{j in block_i} exp(x[j] - local_max_i) * exp(local_max_i - global_max) - = sum_i exp(local_max_i - global_max) * sum_{j in block_i} exp(x[j] - local_max_i) - = sum_i exp(local_max_i - global_max) * local_sum_i So: global_sum = sum_i(local_sum_i * exp(local_max_i - global_max)) And the output for element j in block i is: - y[j] = exp(x[j] - global_max) / global_sum - = exp(x[j] - local_max_i) * exp(local_max_i - global_max) / global_sum This is correct. In kernel 2, I can either: a) Re-read X and compute exp(x[j] - global_max) directly (simpler) b) Use the stored local values (more complex, requires storing exp values) Option (a) is simpler and the traffic is the same (one read of X). Let me go with (a). Now, there's a concern about the Kahan summation in the merge. The merge loop in kernel 2 needs to reduce `num_blocks` values. For batch=4, vocab=262144, BLOCK=2048, num_blocks=128. The loop has 128 iterations, each reading one (max, sum) pair from scratch. This is ~1 KB of data per row, which fits in L1 cache. But each of the 512 programs does this reduction independently. So the scratch data is read 4 times (once per block in each row). This is fine because it's from L1 cache. Actually, for each row, there are `num_blocks` programs, and each reads all `num_blocks` (max, sum) pairs. So the total scratch reads are `batch * num_blocks * num_blocks * 2` fp32 values = 4 * 128 * 128 * 2 * 4 = 512 KB. This is a lot of L1 cache traffic but negligible compared to the X and Y traffic. OK, let me code this up. Here's the refined design: ```python @triton.jit def _block_stats_kernel( X_ptr, stats_scratch, # stats_scratch: [batch * num_blocks, 2] (max, sum) batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=-float("inf")) local_max = tl.max(x) e = tl.exp(x - local_max) local_sum = tl.sum(e) # sum of exp(x - local_max) tl.store(stats_scratch + pid * 2, local_max) tl.store(stats_scratch + pid * 2 + 1, local_sum) @triton.jit def _output_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, stats_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # --- Reduce stats to get global max and Kahan-compensated global sum --- global_max = -float("inf") for i in range(num_blocks): lm = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2) global_max = tl.maximum(global_max, lm) global_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): lm = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2) ls = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2 + 1) corrected = ls * tl.exp(lm - global_max) y = corrected - kc t = global_sum + y kc = (t - global_sum) - y global_sum = t inv = 1.0 / global_sum # --- Compute output --- start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) y = tl.exp(x - global_max) * inv tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, y, mask=m) ``` Wait, I just realized that in the block_stats_kernel, I need to handle the case where `x` is masked out (other=-float("inf")). The `local_max` would be -inf for a fully masked block, and `exp(x - local_max) = exp(-inf - (-inf)) = exp(NaN) = NaN`. But this only happens when `vocab` is not a multiple of BLOCK and the last block has no valid elements. In practice, vocab is a power of 2 (4096, 32768, 131072, 262144) and BLOCK is 2048, so num_blocks = vocab/BLOCK is an integer. No partial blocks. But to be safe, let me handle this. If the block is fully masked (start >= vocab), the program should return early or handle it gracefully. Actually, with `other=-float("inf")`, the masked elements have value -inf. So `local_max` considers only the valid elements (since max ignores -inf among real values). And `exp(x - local_max)` for masked elements gives `exp(-inf - local_max) = 0`. And `local_sum` would be the sum of exp values for valid elements (since 0 from masked elements doesn't contribute). This is correct! But wait, what if all elements in a block are masked? Then `local_max = -inf` and `exp(x - local_max) = exp(-inf + inf) = NaN`. And `local_sum = NaN`. This would propagate NaN through the merge. To handle this, I should skip blocks where `local_max == -inf`. In the output kernel's merge loop, I can skip such blocks: ```python for i in range(num_blocks): lm = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2) ls = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2 + 1) if lm != -float("inf"): # skip empty blocks corrected = ls * tl.exp(lm - global_max) ... ``` But in practice, this won't happen with our shapes. Let me not worry about it. Actually, hmm, I realize there's still a concern about the block_stats_kernel when masked elements are present. Let me trace through: For the last block of a row where vocab is not a multiple of BLOCK: - Some elements are valid, some are masked (other=-inf) - local_max = max of valid elements (correct, -inf from masked doesn't affect max) - exp(x - local_max) for valid elements: correct - exp(x - local_max) for masked elements: exp(-inf - local_max) = 0 - tl.sum(e): sum of correct exp values + 0s = correct sum So this is actually fine. The block_stats_kernel handles partial blocks correctly. OK, let me implement the full solution with the two-kernel approach. Wait, actually I want to also consider whether the two inner loops in the output kernel (for reducing max and sum) will be a performance bottleneck. Each loop has `num_blocks` iterations. For num_blocks=128: - Max loop: 128 loads of fp32 from scratch (512 bytes per row) - Sum loop: 128 * 2 loads of fp32 from scratch (1024 bytes per row) + 128 exp computations The 128 exp computations might be significant. Can I avoid them? Alternative: instead of storing (local_max, local_sum), store (local_max, local_sum * exp(local_max - ???)). But we don't know the global max yet. Or: in the output kernel, first reduce to find global_max, then reduce to find global_sum. The sum loop needs `exp(lm - global_max)` for each block, which requires the global_max. So two separate loops are necessary. Can I fuse the two loops? In each iteration, read (lm, ls), update global_max, and accumulate to global_sum. But the sum depends on the final global_max, so I can't accumulate until I know the final max. Unless I use the online approach for the merge itself: ``` global_max = -inf global_sum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): lm = load(max_scratch[i]) ls = load(sum_scratch[i]) old_max = global_max global_max = max(global_max, lm) corr = exp(old_max - global_max) global_sum *= corr kc *= corr corrected = ls * exp(lm - global_max) y = corrected - kc t = global_sum + y kc = (t - global_sum) - y global_sum = t ``` This fuses the max and sum reduction into a single loop! And it's the online approach applied to the merge. With Kahan, this should be numerically accurate. Let me use this approach. One loop with online merge. Here's the updated output kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _output_kernel( X_ptr, Y_ptr, stats_scratch, batch, vocab, num_blocks, stride_xb, stride_xv, stride_yb, stride_yv, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) row = pid // num_blocks blk = pid % num_blocks if row >= batch: return # Online merge of per-block (max, sum) stats gmax = -float("inf") gsum = 0.0 kc = 0.0 for i in range(num_blocks): lm = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2) ls = tl.load(stats_scratch + (row * num_blocks + i) * 2 + 1) old = gmax gmax = tl.maximum(gmax, lm) corr = tl.exp(old - gmax) gsum = gsum * corr kc = kc * corr corrected = ls * tl.exp(lm - gmax) y = corrected - kc t = gsum + y kc = (t - gsum) - y gsum = t inv = 1.0 / gsum # Write output start = blk * BLOCK cols = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) c = start + cols m = c < vocab x = tl.load(X_ptr + row * stride_xb + c * stride_xv, mask=m, other=0.0) y = tl.exp(x - gmax) * inv tl.store(Y_ptr + row * stride_yb + c * stride_yv, y, mask=m) ``` This is cleaner. One loop for the merge, then one load-compute-store for the output. Now, the total grid size for each kernel is `batch * num_blocks`. For batch=4, vocab=262144, BLOCK=2048: - num_blocks = 128 - Grid: 512 programs per kernel For batch=32, vocab=4096, BLOCK=2048: - num_blocks = 2 - Grid: 64 programs per kernel For batch=16, vocab=32768, BLOCK=2048: - num_blocks = 16 - Grid: 256 programs per kernel These are all reasonable grid sizes for the RTX PRO 6000. +"""Numerically tight softmax via Triton with parallel block decomposition.
 
-Two-pass approach:
-  Pass 1 (online): single scan over the row to find the running max and a
-                   Kahan-compensated sum of exp(x - running_max).
-  Pass 2:          re-read x, write exp(x - final_max) / final_sum.
+Two-kernel design for high GPU utilization:
 
-Total DRAM traffic: 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y = 3 * N * 4 bytes,
-which is optimal for numerically stable softmax (must see the whole row
-before the max is known, then must write normalized output).
+  Kernel 1 (block_stats): One program per (row, col_block). Each finds its
+             local max and sum-of-exp(x - local_max), writes to scratch.
+  Kernel 2 (output):       One program per (row, col_block). Merges all
+             per-block stats via online reduction with Kahan compensation,
+             then writes exp(x - global_max) / global_sum.
+
+Total DRAM traffic: 2 reads of X + 1 write of Y — same as the serial online
+approach, but with batch*ceil(vocab/BLOCK) programs for full SM utilization.
